==PAGE:1==
Experimental investigation of cooling
potential by diﬀuse ventilation
Ismael Ahmad Karayem s123679 and Henrik Bredahl Kristensen s123932
Supervisor: Christian Anker Hviid
June 22, 2018
==PAGE:2==
I.
Summary
This diﬀuse ventilation investigation had taken place at the Technical University of Den-
mark.
The investigation consisted of 10 scenarios with low- and high load within the
interval of 15W/m2 - 72W/m2. The setup in the room had been a landscaped oﬃce and a
meeting room. The indoor environmental quality (IEQ) had been tested according to the
parameters; air velocity, temperature and draught. The parameters were assessed accord-
ing to the DS/EN standard 1752[20]. The Archimedes number had also been applied to
specify the driving forces in the room. The main purpose of the investigation was to exper-
imentally investigate the limitations and possibilities of the diﬀuse ventilation principle in
cooling situations. To support the understanding of the ﬂuid dynamic in the room, CFD
models had been developed. The investigations in the 21.6m2 room was executed with a
fan coil mounted in the plenum, which maintained the cooling, while a box ventilator unit
outside the chamber kept the atmospheric indoor climate. The investigation resulted in;
no signiﬁcant deviation by increasing the amount of exhaust diﬀusers. Applying sound
absorbing plates (extra bass) in the plenum had a minor eﬀect on the IEQ, but for one
experimental layout the categorisation changed from C to B[20]. The ﬂow distribution was
highly depended on the buoyancy forces. The load in the room eﬀected the ﬂow regime.
For the high load meeting room scenarios, the load from the equipment became the ma-
jority of the room. The obtained majority resulted in a more unexpected ﬂow pattern
in the room. The applied loads in this investigation was enough to trigger some of the
limitation of the diﬀuse ceiling ventilation principle, according to DS/EN 1752[20].
i
==PAGE:3==
II.
Preface
This master thesis was executed as a ﬁnal project at the civil engineering study at the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in the department of Civil Engineering. The
project was weighted as 2 x 30 ETCS points.
The project period was from the 22nd
of January to the 22nd of June 2018.
The authors of the project are Henrik Bredahl
Kristensen and Ismael Ahmad Karayem. The two students were supervised by professor
Christian Anker Hviid. The almost unassisted experimental investigation of the project
had taken place in building 119 at DTU.
Until now the existing knowledge about the combination between diﬀuse ceiling ventilation
and indoor climate are limited. So far the investigation had lead to some great beneﬁts,
when using the diﬀuse ventilation principle. However, the manufactures of diﬀuse ceiling
are still missing/interested in speciﬁc experimental investigation of their products. In this
project the tested ceiling had been sponsored by the company Saint-Gobain, which was
praiseworthy for us.
This project had sometimes been challenging and therefore we would like to thank our
supervisor, DTU ICIEE, the staﬀin the workshop and companies, which had sponsored
and supplied equipment to fulﬁll the project. The sponsors are: Saint-Gobain, Lindab,
AGA, Heatcom and Campus Service.
Approved:
Henrik Bredahl Krisensen, M.Sc.

Ismael Ahmad Karayem, M.Sc.

==PAGE:11==
1.
Introduction
1.1
Purpose
The focus on achieving a suitable indoor environmental quality (IEQ) in buildings and
especially in schools, has been increased through the last couple of years. The increased
focus is due to the new knowledge and the importance of having a suitable IEQ[26].
Unfortunately the consequences of having an unacceptable indoor climate is something,
which can be hard to quantify and measure. However, investigations had shown, that an
unacceptable indoor climate tend to aﬀect the productivity, sick leave, social interaction,
health and ability to learn. These results had been obtained by performing several tests
on humans solving e.g. diﬀerent kind of puzzles, reading tests, questionnaires etc..
Compared to other Scandinavian countries, Denmark is the country with the highest
amount of schools with an unacceptable indoor climate[24]. Fixing a problem with an
unacceptable IEQ can be many things. According to this master thesis, it is to investi-
gate alternative ventilation principles for rooms with high internal loads, which tends to
be schools and oﬃce buildings. The ventilation principle in question is ”diﬀuse ceiling
ventilation”, which has some great abilities to maintain a suitable indoor climate for the
occupants in rooms with a high internal load. Furthermore the principle has a low pressure
loss, which is beneﬁcial for the energy consumption, which is highly discussed nowadays.
1.2
Aim of the project
The main task is experimental investigation of the diﬀuse ventilation potential by per-
forming full scale experiments. The full scale investigation will take place in the climate
chamber in building 119 at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The chamber is
constructed of a hollow deck and slab, which causes it to be a thermal heavy building.
The investigation of the principle will cover diﬀerent load patterns, which are presented
by diﬀerent setups of a landscaped oﬃce and a meeting room. The room will be applied
with one or two exhaust diﬀusers connected to a fan coil. The variation of exhausts makes
it possible to investigate its impact on the ﬂow distribution in the room. Furthermore
sound absorbing plates will be installed in the plenum and their eﬀect on the IEQ will be
quantiﬁed. All investigations will be compared by measuring: Mean air velocity, temper-
ature, draught and ventilation eﬃciency (tracer gas). These measured parameters will be
1
==PAGE:12==
CHAPTER 1.
INTRODUCTION
1.3. HYPOTHESES, SCENARIOS AND CASES
evaluated according to the standard DS/EN 1752[20].
The project will also require development of computational ﬂuid dynamic (CFD) models.
The models gives the group the opportunity to identify tendencies, which can be compared
to the experimental part of this project.
Additionally, to enhance the quality of the full scale experiment, it was chosen to produce
eight thermal manikins. Due to the large workload pr. manikin the practical production
had to be made by the group members. The manikins were made with inspiration to the
existing ones in DTU ICIEE (International Centre for Indoor Environment and Energy),
however the internal heat production has to be innovated.
1.3
Hypotheses, scenarios and cases
The main investigation hypothesis for the project is:
What is the limitations and possibilities of diﬀuse ventilation in cooling situ-
ations.
This main hypothesis will be answered by support from the under hypotheses:
1. The ﬂow regime in the room is aﬀected by having two exhaust diﬀusers instead of
one.
2. Applying sound absorbing materials in the plenum like extra bass plates will not
aﬀect IEQ.
3. The ﬂow regime in the room is stronger dependent on the buoyancy forces, than the
inertia forces.
4. Will the load in the room aﬀect the ﬂow distribution.
To investigate the hypotheses, 10 diﬀerent experimental scenarios had taken place. The
scenarios were two diﬀerent cases being a landscaped oﬃce and a meeting room. The
planed scenarios are listed at table 1.1 and 1.2.

2.
Method
2.1
Conceptual
This section serves as an elaboration of how the test facility was used to accomplish the
experiments. Furthermore the applied components and operation of such are also included.
2.1.1
Operation of the facility
The ﬁgure 2.1 below is a drawing presenting how the test facility had been operated. The
cooling was supplied to the climate chamber through a water based heating/cooling unit,
which is illustrated by a separate PI-diagram in section 2.1.2 Heating/cooling unit at ﬁgure
2.2. The water based cooling circuit was then connected to the fan coil, which was installed
above the suspended ceiling. The fan coil supplied the cooling to the climate chamber
through a heat exchanger. The air was exhausted by the diﬀusers below the suspended
ceiling and supplied above. The ﬂow through the fan coil was adjusted manually by a
frequency controller.
Furthermore the facility was also equipped with a box ventilator, which was used to
represent the necessary ﬂow rate for achieving a suﬃcient atmospheric air quality. The
ﬂow rate was controlled by a frequency controller. The box ventilator recirculated the air
from the climate chamber and was equipped with a cooling coil, however the cooling coil
was not used because of control issues.
4
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.1.
CONCEPTUAL
Figure 2.1: PI-diagram showing the ventilation system and fan coil incl. heating/cooling unit.
5
==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.1.
CONCEPTUAL
2.1.2
Heating/cooling unit
As previously mentioned a fan coil was installed within the suspended ceiling. The fan
coil was equipped with a water to air heat exchanger, which was able to supply heating or
cooling depending on the desired application. The ”water-based” supply for the fan coil
was provided from a heating/cooling unit, which was connected as a secondary circuit of
the district cooling.
Figure 2.2 below is an illustration of how the heating/cooling unit was functioning. As
shown the unit consist of a primary (district) and a secondary (internal) circuit, which was
separated by a heat exchanger. This was done in order to limit the risk of leakage, because
this would aﬀect the whole district cooling system. The primary side of the system was
controlled by a temperate valve (AVTA-10) and a balancing valve. Together these two
valves secures the right supply temperature at the internal circuit. The supply temperature
of the district cooling was ∼-8 to -12◦C. In order to achieve a steady temperature, the
internal circuit was equipped with two recirculation loops, being one for the cooling and
one for the heating element. Even though these loops were installed, the cooling supply was
still a bit unsteady. The solution was therefore to lower the internal cooling temperature
below the desired output temperature by ∼1◦C, followed by a minor heating process,
because this resulted in a more steady output temperature, leaving the recirculation loops.
The loops were then followed by three serial-connected circulation pumps. These pumps
works as a ﬂexibility to overcome additional external pressure loss (in our case no more
than two pumps were activated). After the pumps the mass ﬂow was measured by a ”high
accuracy” ﬂow-meter and adjusted manually by the ﬂow valve. Now the chilled liquid was
delivered to the fan coil.
The safety of the heating/cooling unit was handled by the safety valve, which blows
oﬀ, if the internal water pressure increases above 2.5 bars. Furthermore the system was
equipped with a pressure-guard, which secured the system from running, if the pressure
became below a deﬁned pressure. In this way e.g. pumps would not get damaged from
not being cooled by the water.
6
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.1.
CONCEPTUAL
Figure 2.2: PI-diagram showing the heating/cooling unit, which is connected to the fan coil.
2.1.3
Climate chamber
The test facility consists of a chamber, constructed of prefabricated elements with a hol-
low core concrete deck and slab. The walls was made of light material being an insulated
gypsum wall. The test facility was thereby designed as a typical room in a multistory
building. Furthermore the chamber was surrounded by a guard box, which separated the
facility from the rest of the laboratory. The chamber was equipped with heating/cooling
pipes cast into the deck and slab, so the thermal boundary conditions could be controlled.
Figure 2.3 presents how the heating/cooling pipes were installed in the chamber. Fur-
thermore a gab between the guard box and the surfaces of the facility was retained. The
gab between the laboratory ﬂoor and the deck had two caloriferes installed, which made
it possible to circulate the air in the inner core, which resulted in a more or less uniform
surrounding temperature.
7
==PAGE:18==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.1.
CONCEPTUAL
Figure 2.3: Illustration of the thermo active building system in the chamber.
The ﬂoor area of the test facility was 21.6m2, with the geometry 3.6m x 6.0m x 3.6m (W
x L x H). The exterior dimensions of the facility were 4.6m X 7.6m X 5.4m (W x L x H).
The construction of the hollow concrete slabs is presented at ﬁgure 2.4. The ﬁgure also
shows the placement of the heating/cooling pipes. Due to the application of the hollow
concrete slabs, the thermal mass becomes ”heavy”, which categorises the chamber as a
heavy building.
Figure 2.4: Illustration of the hollow thermo active concrete slab.
To control the thermal conditions of the chamber, the thermo active slabs needed to be
supplied with either heating or cooling, dependent on the desired condition. The control
of these conditions were done from a computer, which was electrically connected to the
system.
The installed pipe system is illustrated by ﬁgure 2.5 below.
The cooling supply was
handled by a connection to the DTU district cooling network. The district cooling was
separated from the additional system by a heat exchanger.
The main reason for the
heat exchanger was to limit the risk of leakage of the district cooling network. At the
primary side of the heat exchanger a mixing loop was installed. The mixing loop had two
beneﬁcial possibilities. The ﬁrst one was, that it could control the return temperature of
the primary circuit by recirculating the water from the district cooling, until a suﬃcient
return temperature was obtained. Secondly the mixing loop made it possible to control the
8
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.1.
CONCEPTUAL
eﬀect supplied to the heat exchanger, by the means of controlling the supply temperature
of the primary circuit to the heat exchanger.
At the secondary side of the heat exchanger (central water system) the ﬂuid was divided
into three systems being: Guard (calorifere), deck and slab. The three individual systems
were designed to create the required temperature for each circuit in the chamber. The
heating of the individual loop was supplied by a 60L electrical hot water tank.
The
electrical heating element was controlled by the main computer, as a function of the
desired supply temperature to the given loop. Each individual loop was also equipped
with a mixing loop, which made it possible to ”disconnect” the cooling circuit if it was
not needed.
The beneﬁt of using these hot water boilers as supply temperature storage, was that it
made the system less sensitive to ﬂuctuations of e.g. control ability of the valves and
heating elements. However, through proper control these ﬂuctuations could be neglected.
Unfortunately an electronic error prevented the computer to control the mixing valves in
the circuit, and therefore they could not be used. However, the fans in the calorifere were
still applied in order to obtain an uniform surrounding temperature, ﬂowing in the guard
box.

2.1.4.9
Safety valve
Whenever water is heated, it is a requirement to have a safety valve installed. An example
of a safety valve is shown at ﬁgure 2.14. The heating process causes the density of the
water to decrease, meaning that the volume of the given amount of water increases. By
having the process in a closed loop, the result is an increased pressure in the system. Due
to the pressure limits in pipe systems, it is therefore a safety requirement to install a safety
valve, which can gradually decrease the pressure in the system. The safety valve must not
14
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.1.
CONCEPTUAL
be blocked and should always be connected to a drain, to minimise the risk of personal
damage by scalding. The safety valve for a central water system will typically open at a
pressure of 2.5 bar.
Figure 2.14: Safety valve.
2.1.4.10
Expansion vessel
As previously mentioned the volume of the water varies with respect to the temperature.
Because of the safety valve the system will be kept at no more than the release pressure
e.g. 2.5 bar at the highest occurring temperature and volume. However, when the system
runs at lower temperatures the reduced thermal expansion might locally drain the system.
To compensate for that, an expansion vessel is required. An example of an expansion
vessel is shown at ﬁgure 2.15.
Figure 2.15: Expansion vessels.
2.1.4.11
Mass ﬂow meter
A mass ﬂow meter was used to determine the water ﬂow of the liquid. The mass ﬂow meter
gives an electrical current [mA] as the output. By applying an equation, it is possible to
calculate the ﬂow through the mass ﬂow meter, with a high accuracy. The mass ﬂow meter
is presented at ﬁgure 2.16.

inside the core of the manikin to secure an uniform distribution of the internal heat.

2.2. PHYSICAL
2.2
Physical
In this chapter the most dominating phenomenons from the conceptual model will be
described.
2.2.1
Chamber
When designing the chamber it was necessary to evaluate the chosen construction material,
since it would have an impact on the thermal parameters. By knowing the material and
dimensions of such, the overall heat transfer coeﬃcient (U-value) could be calculated, in
order to asses the energy balance of the chamber. As previously mentioned the chamber
consists of a concrete slab and deck. Even though the four vertical walls were made of
gypsum, the high share of concrete, made the chamber act as a thermally heavy building.
This gives the possibility of releasing or storing the accumulated heat in the surfaces. This
could be suﬃcient for reducing the amount of energy used for cooling. However, in the test
facility the thermal mass resulted in a signiﬁcant waiting time for achieving a steady state
condition. Furthermore the guard box, which encloses the chamber consists of insulated
aluminium plates. The chamber was placed inside a laboratory. Thereby the surrounding
temperature for the guard box had not been lower than ∼21◦C (summer season).
From an acoustical perspective, the inside of the chamber consists of a mix between hard
and soft surfaces. The ﬂoor was constructed of wood plates, while the walls were con-
structed of gypsum plates and the suspended ceiling of glass wool. The acoustical envi-
ronment was beneﬁted from the materials used in the suspended ceiling, because it worked
as a sound absorber between the control volumes above and below the suspended ceiling.
2.2.2
Fan coil
By having a fan coil installed above the suspended ceiling, it was possible to cool the
recirculated air in the room. A ﬂex duct was connected between the extract diﬀuser and
the fan coil. The extracted air was forced through the fan coil, where it passed a coil,
from where the air was chilled by forced convection. The air was blown through the fan
coil by an internal fan. A ﬁlter installed at the extract, separated the small dust particles
from the recirculated air.
17
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.2. PHYSICAL
2.2.3
Ventilation unit
The chamber could only be supplied with recirculated air by the box ventilation unit,
installed outside the guard box. The air was forced through the system by a backward
curved fan.
The air was supplied above the suspended ceiling, while the exhaust was
located beneath. Both duct connections were installed at the same wall.
2.2.4
Plate heat exchanger
The ﬂuid supplied from the district cooling at DTU enters a plate heat exchanger. Inside
the heat exchanger a heat ﬂux was transferred from the warm secondary side to the cold
primary side. Each mass ﬂow was controlled in order to give the desired temperature at
the secondary side. The ﬂuid in the primary side consisted of a glycol-water mixture,
while the secondary side consisted of demineralised water. The demineralised water was
chosen in order to keep the density of the water, with respect to the already calibrated
mass ﬂow meter. Therefore it was extremely important to consider the temperature, in
order to avoid the risk of a frost burst. Both mass ﬂows were considered to be turbulent
inside the heat exchanger, which resulted in the best conditions for a heat transfer.
2.2.5
Heat sources
The presence of heat sources in the chamber eﬀected the air movement. If the temperature
of the heat sources was warmer than the surrounding air, the air was heated, causing an
upstream due to density. If an air movement is primary caused by the heat sources, the
phenomena is said to be deﬁned by the buoyancy depended ﬂow, which can be assessed
by the Archimedes number[14].
18
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD

2.3
Mathematical

namic and indoor climate analysis are also presented.
2.3.1
Chamber
The heat loss from the chamber to the surroundings can be explained by equation 2.1[3].
qloss = U · As · (Ts −Ti)
(2.1)
Where:

As: Surface area [m2]
Ts: Surrounding temperature [K]
Ti: Indoor temperature [K]
The overall heat transfer coeﬃcient in equation 2.1 can be determined by equation 2.2.
The applied equation is based on having a heat transfer rate through a plane wall[3].

The ﬂuid ﬂowing through the heat exchanger is cooled by passing a compact ”ﬁn and

2.4.
NUMERICAL
2.4
Numerical
This section contains the information concerning the CFD models build in Flovent.
2.4.1
Consideration and layout
Before any practical investigation of the ventilation principle CFD models were developed.
By using simulations, it would be possible for the group to investigate diﬀerent cases,
obtain knowledge and save time for the full scale experiments. To make it possible to
compare the CFD models with the practical investigations, the layout had to be similar.
However the CFD models were made with minor deviations, in order to e.g. favour the
grid system. The rules applied for the grid system is shown at table 2.2. In order to
obtain similarity, the applied heat gains were the same as the ones applied in the full scale
experiments. When implementing the heat gains into the CFD models, they should be
divided into the respective categories; radiant- and convective heat transfer rates. The
separation was done, because the radiant heat transfer rate aﬀects the room slowly. The
radiant heat transfer rate is ﬁrst transferred to the surrounding surfaces through radiation.
Afterwards it is released as convective heat. The heat transfer rate is typically divided by
allocating 60% as radiant- and 40% as convective heat transfer rate[14]. The separation
of radiant- and convective heat is shown at table 2.1. The convective heat gains were
attached to the respective ”cuboids” in the CFD models, while the radiant heat gains in
Flovent were attached to the walls, by increasing the surface temperature. As a quality
control, the total heat gain in the CFD models should comply with the total heat gain
listed in table 1.1 and 1.2.
Table 2.1: Heat gains.
-
Radiant [W]
Convective [W]
Total [W]
Torso
30
20
50
Head
15
10
25
Legs
3
2
5
PC
36
24
60
Projector
300
200
500
2.4.2
Assumptions
To make a good assessment of the CFD models, it was important to take the assumptions
into consideration. The applied assumptions are listed below:
25
==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.4.
NUMERICAL
• Incompressible ﬂuid.
• Stationary ratio between latent and sensible heat transfer rate from the manikins.
• Constant viscosity.
• The radiant heat ﬂux was assigned to the envelope.
• The ratio between radiant and convective heat transfer was 60%/40% for the applied
loads.
• The geometrical deviations between the CFD and the full scales experiment e.g.
design of legs are neglected.
• The attempt to design the diﬀuse ceiling by using the ”perforated plate” application
from Flovent.
2.4.3
Solver
The parameters air velocity, pressure and temperature varies in a stochastic way. Therefore
a turbulence model was needed for solving the planned cases. The available solvers for
turbulence models in Flovent were: Capped, Algebraic and K-epsilon. The applied model
for the project was the K-epsilon model. The model is known for being a two equation
model, which means it consists of two transported variables.
The ﬁrst variable is the
turbulence energy [k], while the second variable is the rate of dissipation of turbulence
energy [ϵ]. The model is one of the most commonly used, but it has some limitations.
The model requires modiﬁcations near the walls to obtain a low Reynolds number, while
the second limit is that it over-predicts shear stress in adverse pressure ﬂows because of
too large length scale, due to too low dissipation[15]. When applying the k-epsilon model
for simulation purposes the equations mentioned in section 2.3.4 Numerical equations for
Flovent were applied together with an extra mathematical expression, that included the
variables k and ϵ.
2.4.4
Grid
When making a CFD model the grid size has to be carefully considered. The reason is
that a decrease in the grid, will increases the simulation time of the model. The chosen
approach for the grid design of the given models, was therefore the ”multiblock grid”.
The rationale behind the multiblock grid, is to locally divide the volumes into ﬁne grids,
whereas other less relevant volumes will be kept in a more coarse grid size.
The structure of the grid is also important, because some grid systems like a tetrahe-
dral system would be a better match for non-linear structures. The manikins applied in
26
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.4.
NUMERICAL
the CFD models were made to match the ones applied in the test facility. However, the
manikins applied in the CFD models were only constructed of rectangular shapes, whereas
the manikins applied for the full scale experiment were constructed of round and rectan-
gular geometries. In order to reduce the deviations, the surface area was calculated to be
the same for each body part. Due to the non-existing curved structures in the simulation
model, it was assumed that the rectangular grid system would be a suﬃcient match.
Each grid is calculated as a control volume. By having many control volumes the accuracy
of the simulated model becomes better. However, an increasing number of grids results
in a required increase in CPU capacity for the computer. Therefore the chamber had
to be designed according to some kind of rationale. The speciﬁc design rules applied for
development of the CFD models are shown at table 2.2 below. The reason for the given
rules was to secure an acceptable performance and accuracy of the results.
Table 2.2: Grid rules for the CFD models[14].

2.4.5.1
Enclosure
The enclosure was made in the same dimensions as the full scale chamber, being 3.6m x
6.0m x 3.6m (W x L x H). In the CFD model the enclosure was functioning as a whole,
meaning it was not possible to assign the speciﬁc ”Overall heat transfer coeﬃcient” (U-
value) to each wall. Therefore it was chosen to apply a weighted average of the three
diﬀerent U-values. Table 2.3 shows the calculated U-value for each wall. By taking the
area into consideration the calculated weighted average was calculated to 0.90 (W/(m2·K).
The calculation of the U-values was previously done by two students, by using the software
”Rockwool energy design”[18]. The applied U-values were taken from their report[16] and
cross-checked before appliance.
29
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.4.

Type
Heat transfer coeﬃcient
Wall
0.38 W/(m2·K)
Deck
1.32 W/(m2·K)

2.15 W/(m2·K)
2.4.5.2
Box ventilator unit
The supply and exhaust from the box ventilator unit was modelled as a ”ﬁxed ﬂow” object.
The attributes for both ﬁxed ﬂows were similar to the ones in the full scale experiment,
but with the distinction of the duct geometry. To secure the same velocity, the hydraulic
diameter was calculated to be identical. The calculation made for this task is attached in
the appendix section 9.1.1 Calculation of the diameter for the ventilation duct for the CFD
models. Despite of the full scale experiment, the setup of the box ventilation unit was
made without recirculation. The ﬂow rate was set only to maintain an atmospheric indoor
air quality. The temperature of the supply air was set to match the ambient temperature.
2.4.5.3
Fan coil
The fan coil was modelled as a ”recirculation device”. By using the object ”recirculation
device”, it required an attachment of a cuboid, in order to function. The dimensions of
the cuboid had to be identical to the fan coil applied in the full scale experiment. The ﬂow
rate and heat transfer rate of the fan coil were adjusted to match the diﬀerent loads, with
respect to the given scenario. For the scenarios where two exhausts were connected to the
fan coil, two recirculation devices were applied in the model. Furthermore the opening
area and the heat transfer rate were divided between them.
2.4.5.4
Measurement points
To extract data from the CFD models, it was necessary to clarify the coordinates of the
locations where the data should be collected from. A measurement point was inserted
to the model as an object. The coordinates were typed into the inserted object ”mon-
itor point”.
The determined measurement points were 30cm behind each occupant in
the heights; 0.1m, 0.6m, 1.1m, 1.7m, 2.2m, 2.4m, 2.6m and 2.8m above the ﬂoor level.
The rationale behind the distance of 30cm, was to avoid the eﬀect from the plume. To
collect data from each surface in the chamber, six monitor points were attached to the
surfaces below and above the suspended ceiling. Furthermore the chamber was covered

presented at ﬁgure 2.22 and 2.23. The data exported from each point consists of: Air
velocity, temperature and concentration of pollutant.

2.4.5.5
Heat sources
The heat sources were constructed of cuboids. In the full scale experiment, some of the
heat sources were constructed of circular geometries. The appliance of cuboids required
a balancing of the external area of the heat sources. The heat sources were: Manikins,
projector, pc’s and the solar gain. The convective heat transfer rates presented at table
2.1 were attached to the heat sources. The design of the heat sources is presented at ﬁgure
2.24.
Figure 2.24: The designed heat sources in CFD. In the order: Manikin, projector, pc and solar gain.
2.4.5.6
CO2
To make it possible to calculate the ventilation eﬃciencies, it required tracer gas experi-
ments. The applied tracer gas was CO2. The amount of gas applied in the model was with
respect to the amount of CO2 in the atmospheric air and the rate of a seated person. The
CO2 coming from the atmosphere was emitted in the supply duct of the box ventilation
unit (the ﬁxed ﬂow object). The CO2 emitted from the occupants, were released in front
of each occupant, at the location of the chin.
2.4.5.7
Suspended ceiling
The suspended ceiling was constructed by applying a cuboid for each ceiling plate. The
gab between the plates was where the air was supplied to the chamber. The air gabs were
constructed by perforated plates. The diameter of the holes in the perforated plates was
calculated to match the estimated opening area of the applied plates from the full scale
experiment. After designing a given opening area, the ceiling was simulated and followed
by a comparison to the velocities measured in the full scale experiment. The results of the
32

METHOD
2.4.
NUMERICAL
ﬁnal adjustment are shown at ﬁgure 2.25 in section 2.4.5.8 Model validation.
2.4.5.8
Model validation
The initial model validation was made by measuring the velocities ∼5cm below the sus-
pended ceiling, at 15 positions, illustrated at ﬁgure 2.26. As shown at ﬁgure 2.25, the
velocities from the CFD model had minor deviations from the measured values. The most
critical deviation is shown at position number four. The reason for the high velocity in the
full scale experiment, might be that the measurement was executed close to the extract
from the fan coil. Besides of the deviation in position number four, the tendencies were
assessed to be similar. Therefore it was possible to conclude that the CFD models, could
be applied to investigate tendencies, but it might not be reliable on absolute values.
Figure 2.25: Model validation.
33
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 2.
METHOD
2.4.
NUMERICAL
Figure 2.26: Positions that were used to validate the CFD model.
When simulating the indoor climate by CFD software, it is a typical problem that the
models have a tendency of not converging, when performing a steady state simulation.
The reason is because of the developed plume from e.g. the occupants. Therefore it was
necessary to implement a transient solution after simulating the steady state solution. The
appliance of the transient simulation resulted in a convergence of all models, meaning that
all of the residuals being; velocities, pressure, temperature, concentration, DissTurb and
KETurb converged.

3.1.5
Heating/cooling unit
The heating/cooling unit was received without any insulation, which resulted in an unde-
sired energy gain, causing an increase of the temperature. To improve the unit the group
decided to insulate the heat exchanger and as much of the pipe system as possible. The
unit is shown with insulation at ﬁgure 3.12.
41
==PAGE:52==
CHAPTER 3.
EXPERIMENTAL SETUP
3.1.
MODIFICATION OF THE FACILITY
Figure 3.12: The heating/cooling unit with insulation.
The unit had a mixing valve installed at the primary circuit, but since the electrical wire
was disconnected, the ability to control was eliminated. Achieving a stable and controlled
temperature was crucial and therefore the group determined to improve the unit. Therefore
the primary circuit was equipped with a Danfoss AVTA-10 valve followed by a balancing
valve, which solved the problem. The AVTA valve is a valve, which according to the ﬂuid
inside the measuring bulb, closes the valve as a function of an increasing temperature.
The installed valves at the primary circuit are shown at ﬁgure 3.13.

3.2
Thermal manikins
This section serves as an elaboration of the production of the thermal manikins for the
full scale experiment. In order to accomplish the experiment it was essential to have a
disposal of eight thermal manikins. Since this wasn’t the case, a production of these were
necessary. Because of the competences within the group and the limitations of manpower
from the craftsmen, it was chosen to manufacture these manikins as a part of the project.
However, some help from the electricians was acquired for making the correct electrical
installation within the manikins.

Construction of the manikin
The construction was primarily made of round ventilation ducts and ﬁttings. Unfortu-
nately the torso had to be constructed of a rectangular duct. Economically this was not
an option, so therefore the torso and the rectangular caps were made from cutting and
folding steel plates by the group members. Figure 3.14 and 3.15 presents the process of
cutting and folding the torso for the manikins.

3.2.4
Investigation of the thermal manikin
The design of the manikins was determined by the knowledge achieved in the report Impact
of geometry of a sedentary occupant simulator on the generated thermal plume: Experi-
mental investigation. Made by Daria Zukowska, Arsen Melikov and Zbigniew Popiolek.
The conclusion was, that the design of the manikins was suﬃcient for an indoor climate
simulation, because the thermal plume was not strongly concentrated, as if the design was
of a simple rectangular box. Furthermore it could be concluded, ”the results show that the
more humanlike the simulator, the more similar the thermal plume to that above a sitting
person”.[28].
To assure a correct heat rate from the thermal manikins, the voltage was adjusted. By
having a standard voltage of 230 volt, the manikins were calculated and measured to emit
105-106 watt each. In order to deliver ∼80 watt the voltage was lowered to 205 volt. By
having the right heat rate the investigation process was initiated.
The investigation of the manikins was made by photographing the manikins with a thermal
camera and comparing it to a real human. Figure 3.23 and 3.24 presents a thermo photo
47
==PAGE:58==
CHAPTER 3.
EXPERIMENTAL SETUP
3.2.
THERMAL MANIKINS
of a manikin and a human. In the experiment the human was wearing light insulated
clothing, being a t-shirt and shorts. The bare legs from the human are shown to be a
major deviation according to the surface temperature. However, the manikin was only
equipped with heating cable at the thigh region and not the pleat region, which explains
the deviation. The highest temperature occurring for the manikin and the human was
more of less the same with a minor deviation of ∼1◦C.

3.5
Challenges
3.5.1
Thermo active system
The control of the thermo active system had not been working during the project. Initially
it was planned to be ﬁxed, however the electricians could not manage the task and therefore
the system was omitted.
3.5.2
Cooling coil at the box ventilation unit
Unfortunately a wrong assembled mixing loop, caused the control of the heat exchanger to
be extremely sensible to ﬂuctuations of the supply temperature from the district cooling
network. Normally the district cooling network is quite reliable. However, other master
thesis experiments aﬀected the temperature in such a way, that the mixing loop became
unbalanced and unable to reestablish the determined temperature. Therefore the cooling
coil was omitted. However, the mixing circuit presented at ﬁgure 2.1 above, is how it
should have been assembled, in order to function.
54
==PAGE:65==
CHAPTER 3.
EXPERIMENTAL SETUP
3.5. CHALLENGES
3.5.3
Heating/cooling unit
The heating/cooling unit caused several minor and major problems, which had to be
solved. Some of the issues and how they were solved are elaborated in this section.
At the beginning of the project, the heating/cooling unit raised an alarm. The alarm was
caused by a low internal pressure, measured by the RT110 pressure guard, and solved by
increasing the internal pressure.
The next issue was; ﬁrstly a none existing control of the cooling circuit. However, this was
to be handled manually by a ball valve. The issue with the manual control was that the
water in the internal circuit was not protected against frost burst. Secondly, by omitting
the thermal active system, the experiment needed to continue unattended over night, to
achieve steady state conditions. Thirdly the heating/cooling unit needed to be stressed
(risk of frost burst), in order to deliver the desired supply temperature. The combination
of these three issues resulted in a need for control, which the group ﬁxed by ordering and
installing a Danfoss AVTA valve.
Even with the thermo valve installed, the system was still not able to deliver the desired
temperature for the most demanding experiments, without a severe risk of a frost burst.
The lacking energy was therefore handled by insulating the whole pipe loop in the heat-
ing/cooling unit, which was constructed of cobber pipes with a high thermal conductivity.
55
==PAGE:66==
4.
Results
In this section the results of the project are presented together with a suitable description
of the elements or tendencies, which should be noticed.
4.1
Air velocity, temperature and draught
In order to give the best illustration of the measured parameters air velocity, temperature
and draught, it was chosen to divide the chamber into three sectional views, cut in the
width direction. All of the measurements are referred to at a given height and position.
Figure 4.1 presents a top-view illustration of the numbering of the stated positions. The
three sectional views are at ﬁgure 4.1 referred to as view 1-3.
Figure 4.1: Top-view illustration of the numbering of the measurement points and the sectional views.
Furthermore the ﬁgures presenting the results are coloured. The given colour refers to an
assessment of the result with respect to DS/EN 1752[20]. Additionally some of the squares
are ﬁlled with a grey colour, combined with a text: ”N/A”. This means that the given
location had not been measured, because the given arrangement became an obstacle in
the full scale experiments. For the measurements, the colour ﬁll refers to; green=category
A, yellow=category B, blue=category C and red=exceedance of category.
If a graphical illustration should be desired instead of the presented tables below, these
can be accessed in the appendix section 9.2 Data from the full scale experiments and in
section 9.3 Data from the CFD investigations.
To ease the load of reading through several similar elaborations of the results from section
4.1.1 to section 4.1.9, it was chosen to give some general comments to the repeating
tendencies.
56
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 4.
RESULTS
4.1. AIR VELOCITY, TEMPERATURE AND DRAUGHT
Velocity:
The velocities were assessed and categorised according to DS/EN 1752[20] table 1. Based
on the velocities, it was the full scale experiments, which had the highest amount of points
exceeding a category A. When comparing the full scale experiments with the CFD models,
these tendencies were more often present for the scenarios with a four person oﬃce in the
full scale experiments. However, the amount of points exceeding a category A was more
similar, when comparing the full scale experiments with the CFD models for the eight
person meeting room scenarios.
In the high load CFD models, the velocities at and adjacent to position number eight are
shown to be high. The reason is caused by the solar gain, which is placed in that area.
Unfortunately the location of the supply and exhaust from the box ventilation unit are
mounted, so it caused an asymmetry of the ﬂow in the chamber, which was typically shown
by increased velocities at the heights 2.6m and 2.8m above ﬂoor level at position 6 and
7. However, the CFD models were assessed to be most sensitive to the asymmetry. The
statement was justiﬁed by the streamline assessment, presented in section 9.3.1 Streamlines
assessment.
Temperature:
The temperatures were assessed according to the vertical temperature diﬀerence and cat-
egorised according to DS/EN 1752[20] table A.2. The majority of the observed vertical
temperature proﬁles were within a category A. Few positions had points that exceeded
a category A, but these points were near the suspended ceiling and therefore out of the
occupied zone.
The temperature proﬁles for the full scale experiments had the lowest temperatures at the
ankle level and just below the ceiling plates. The CFD models had the same tendency.
However, the temperature diﬀerence between the ankle and the abdomen level was less
than for the full scale experiments. Comparing the temperature proﬁles, above and below
the suspended ceiling, it was shown that the temperature was generally lower for the CFD
models, than for the full scale experiments, below the ceiling. The opposite eﬀect was
shown to be present, when assessing the temperatures above the ceiling plates.
Draught:
The draught rates were assessed and categorised according to DS/EN 1752[20] table A.1.
The draught rate is known to be a product of the air velocity and temperature proﬁles.
The location of draught rates higher than category A, should therefore comply with the
locations where the temperature or the air velocity exceeds the categorised limitation,
which was the case for all scenarios. For the full scale experiments, the air velocity was
generally higher than for the CFD models. Thereby the temperature seemed to by the
dominating factor for draught in CFD models, while the air velocity dominated for the
full scale experiments.

4.2
Archimedes number
When calculating the Archimedes number, two variables had to be determined. The two
variables were the velocity and the temperature of the air entering the room (from the
plenum to the room). This was done by calculating the mean value for the measurement
points at level 2.8m, which was just below the suspended ceiling.
However, this might be a bit too far from the ceiling gap to be representing the inlet
conditions. Therefore the deviation between the mean velocity and the mean temperature
in the height 2.8m above the ﬂoor level and at 0.02m below the suspended ceiling gap
were investigated. The values used for the investigation were assessed by using the CFD
models. The deviations are shown at table 4.2 and 4.3. The deviation was acceptable for
all scenarios besides of scenario; ”8 persons low load meeting room with 1 exhaust” and
87
==PAGE:98==
CHAPTER 4.
RESULTS
4.2.
ARCHIMEDES NUMBER
”8 persons low load meeting room with 2 exhaust”. These scenarios had a high deviation
for the air velocity, which should be taken into account.

1 Velocities measured 0.02m below the suspended ceiling.
2 Velocities measured 2.8m above ﬂoor level.
The assessment of the calculated Archimedes numbers were done according to the quo-
tation listed below. ”Ar, characterizes the ratio of buoyancy forces over inertia forces
for forced ﬂuid ﬂow; a high number indicating highly buoyancy-aﬀected ﬂow”[14]. The
boundary between the buoyancy and the inertia aﬀected ﬂow had been clariﬁed to be: If
the Ar<<1, the ﬂow is dominated by inertia forces (forced convection). If Ar>>1, the
ﬂow is dominated by the buoyancy forces (natural convection)[11].
The ﬁgures below presents the calculated Archimedes numbers for the previously men-
tioned locations and sectional views at ﬁgure 4.1. Each point was ﬁlled with a colour,
which represents if the ﬂow was dominated by buoyancy- (green ﬁll) or inertia forces
88
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 4.
RESULTS
4.2.
ARCHIMEDES NUMBER
(yellow ﬁll). In general the ﬁgures reveals, that the ﬂow was aﬀected by the buoyancy
forces (natural convection). In general the temperature diﬀerences were small (less than
1◦C). The Archimedes number was assessed to have a negative exponential behaviour. By
having ﬂuctuations of either the velocity or the temperature, the Archimedes number is
going towards zero (Ar→0). This means that an optimum point exists between the two
parameters. For the scenarios, the optimum point seamed to be in the middle height of
the chamber below plenum, being at 2.2m and 2.4m.

for all of the points included in the seated interval (0.1m to 1.1m above ﬂoor level). In order
to present the operative temperature for each scenario, the mean value of all operative
temperatures was calculated, which is illustrated by table 4.4.

4.6
Ventilation eﬃciency
The ventilation eﬃciency was calculated to investigate how well the air was mixed in each
of the scenarios. By making the calculations for the full scale experiments and the CFD
models, it was also possible to investigate the deviation between the models.
Table 4.5 presents the calculated values. The ventilation eﬃciency was for the full scale
experiment based on seven measurement locations, whereas the eﬃciency for the CFD
model was based on approximate 90-110 locations, depended on the arrangement of the
chamber.
Generally all of the calculated ventilation eﬃciencies were close to 1, which indicates
the chamber to be fully mixed.
Values just above/below 1, indicates tendencies of a
minor recirculation of the air[19]. Comparing the eﬃciencies obtained in the full scale
experiments and the CFD models, the deviations were of a minor signiﬁcance. The highest
deviation was for the scenario ”8 person low load 1 exhaust”.

4.7
Energy balance of the chamber
The energy balance for each experiment was calculated. The reason was, that the chamber
was measured to receive more energy, than the calculated scenario-based load. Therefore
the additional energy had to be quantiﬁed. Table 4.6 below presents the calculated heat
ﬂux for the scenarios; delivered by the heating/cooling unit (H/C), obtained or emitted
from the envelope, emitted from the fan from the fan coil, obtained or emitted from the
box ventilation unit and lastly the unaccounted heat ﬂux.
When the scenario-based load was compared to the delivered energy, some deviations
occurred. The scenario ”8 person high load meeting room with bass system” had the
largest deviation. The unaccounted energy can have many reasons: The eﬀect of the fan
coil was only measured according to the air temperatures, however the radiation factor
of the frame was not included in the calculation of the heat ﬂux, and might be the one
causing the deviation. Additionally before each measurement, the steady state conditions
of the chamber was visually controlled by assessment of the logged temperatures. If the
temperatures were visually unaﬀected by time, the given scenario was assessed to be at
a steady state. If steady state had not been obtained after all, then this could also have
been aﬀecting the energy balance in the chamber. Lastly the deviations of the measuring
equipment was also a factor.

5.
Discussion
It was desired to investigate if an extra exhaust diﬀuser could minimise or change the ﬂow
distribution in the room, and thereby become a design parameter to avoid discomfort.
This optimistic investigation was executed with diﬀerent heat loads in the chamber. The
rationale was to initiate any major ﬂuctuations between one or two exhaust diﬀusers
connected to the fan coil. Furthermore the investigation was made even though it was
known, that an exhaust diﬀuser only has a slight eﬀect on the air close to the diﬀuser.
When comparing the following parameters; air velocity, temperature, draught and Archimedes
number for the scenarios with one or two exhausts connected to the fan coil, a minimal dif-
ference occurred for the evaluated parameters. Generally, the temperature and Archimedes
number were the most aﬀected parameters across the experiments. By assessing the mean
values, the temperature and Archimedes number had a slight tendency of decreasing, when
having two exhausts instead of one.
Unfortunately the tendencies were more diﬃcult to assess for the ”4 person high load 2
exhaust” and the ”4 person high load 1 exhaust”. The reason was that the group had
to be careful not to get a frost burst of the heat exchanger in the heating/cooling unit.
Thereby the ”4 person high load 1 exhaust” experiment became a benchmark test for the
limitation of the heat exchanger. After having a success in the benchmark test, the group
proceeded with the high load experiments. Unfortunately the benchmark test aﬀected the
ratio between air ﬂow rate and temperature from the fan coil. Thereby the tendencies
between these two experiments were assessed to have a minor deviation, according to the
majority.
By installing the sound absorbing system (extra bass), the supply area of the diﬀuse
ceiling was reduced. By decreasing the supply area, the pressure loss was increased at
the remaining supply area, because of the same volume ﬂow rate. An increased pressure
loss will aﬀect the velocity to decrease, however by simultaneously reducing the supply
area, the eﬀect was thought to be measured by an increased velocity at the remaining
supply area and visa verca. Given that the bass system was installed at the edges of the
room, the velocities at the center of the room were expected to increase. For the four
person scenario, the expected increase in velocity could not be validated, so perhaps the
current ﬂow rate had been to small to aﬀect the velocity. For the eight person scenario the
expected tendency was assessed to be present at some of the measured points, however
the middle of the room was not measured, because of accessibility issues caused by the
arrangement (tables). Another perspective for the missing increase of the air velocity in
the middle of the room, could also be that, the air velocity generated by the plumes were
111
==PAGE:122==
CHAPTER 5.
DISCUSSION
high enough to deaccelerate the increased velocity.
The general evaluation of the parameters; mean air- velocity, temperature, draught and
the Archimedes number, was that the implementation of the extra bass plates had a minor
decreasing eﬀect of the parameters. However, the implementation of extra bass plates was
enough to change the ”4 person high load 1 exhaust” scenario from a category C to a
category B[20].
Furthermore, it could be argued that the assumption of having assigned the velocities at
level 2.8m as the inlet velocities, is a rough decision. When investigating the deviations,
these were below 12%, except for the two ”8 person low load” experiments, which were
calculated to be below 21%. The deviations were assessed in the CFD models by having
15 measuring points located 0.02m below the gap of the ceiling plates. The mean value
of the 15 measured velocities was then compared to the calculated mean velocity mea-
sured at level 2.8m. For the temperatures the highest deviation was no more than 1.3%.
Thereby the assumption of using 2.8m as the inlet conditions, seems to be acceptable
for all scenarios, except for the ”8 person low load” models, which has to be used with
caution.
The diﬀuse ventilation principle is known to be highly dependent on the buoyancy forces[14],
which is a major factor for the ﬂow distribution in the room. By investigating the calcu-
lated Archimedes number in the scenarios, it was shown that the Archimedes number was
larger than 1 in almost all measured points, which indicated, that the ﬂow distribution
was caused by the ”free convection”, generated by the occupants and the heat sources. By
looking at the development of the Archimedes numbers, it was shown that the numbers
increased from the ankle level to the heights 2.2m and 2.4m above ﬂoor level. Furthermore,
when comparing the mean values of the high load scenarios with the low load scenarios,
it showed that the Archimedes number was increasing as the loads were decreasing. This
tendency was expected, since the lower the load, the lower the required ”forced” ﬂow rate,
from the fan coil or the box ventilation unit. This relation was under the assumption of
having an ”all most” unchanging temperature diﬀerence across the experiments, which
was the case for the investigations in question.
By changing the load in the room, it was shown that the parameters air velocity, temper-
ature, draught and Archimedes number were aﬀected. Speciﬁcally the relation was; an
increase of the load in the room, causes an increase of the parameters. At the low load
scenarios, it was shown how the plume accelerated the air upwards from the ankle level to
the ceiling. Furthermore, cyclones between the occupants were developed, which aﬀected
the air distribution. By increasing the load in the four person oﬃce scenarios, it was visu-
alised how the driving forces, caused by the heat sources, contributed to the acceleration of
the air. For the eight person high load scenario, it was showed how the plumes generated
by the equipment, caused a major factor to the ﬂow distribution, rather than the plumes
112
==PAGE:123==
CHAPTER 5.
DISCUSSION
generated by the occupants. Thereby it seemed that the equipment became the majority
for the dynamic ﬂow distribution in the room.
Generally it was assessed that a category A classiﬁcation was easier awarded, by decreasing
the load. However, it should be noticed, that the ventilation principle in question requires
a buoyancy driven ﬂow, in order to create a full mixing and avoid the risk of the polluted
air to reenter the plenum.
As expected, the calculated ventilation eﬃciency showed a tendency of a fully mixed envi-
ronment for all scenarios. However, experiments being lower than 0.90 were not expected,
because the arrangement of the low load model, should had been more than enough to
impose a good mixing of the room. The rationale for this argument, was that the room
were equipped with more buoyancy forced ﬂow, than it would have been by e.g. following
the ”design guide lines” stated in the Danish standard DS/EN 15251[23], which assigns
10m2 pr. person for an oﬃce environment.
When assessing the deviation of the ventilation eﬃciency between the full scale experi-
ments and the CFD models, one of the deviation-factors could be, that the ventilation
eﬃciencies for the full scale experiments were based on a limited amount of measurement
points compared to the CFD models. Investigation of the velocity distribution in the CFD
models showed a formation of cyclones. By having less measurement points in the full
scale experiments, the eﬀect of e.g. recirculation caused by cyclones might not have been
included, which could result in an increase or decrease of the ventilation eﬃciency.
The calculated mean operative temperatures had smaller deviations from one scenario to
another. Only one full scale experiment, complied with a category B, while the rest of the
experiments complied with a category C. The same tendency was assessed for the CFD
models. Even though the operative temperature had not been at a desired category, it
was notable that it managed not to aﬀect the categorisation of the other parameters e.g.
air velocity, temperature, draught etc..
In order to obtain control of the boundary conditions of the full scale experiment, all of the
heat ﬂuxes had to be determined. This were done by setting up measuring equipment e.g.
thermo sensor at several locations. When doing so, some simpliﬁcations had to be made,
which sometimes tends to cause some minor deviations. In the full scale experiment, the
challenge became the energy balance of the room and the achievement of steady state
conditions. Because of an electrical fault, the boundary control system of the chamber
had not been functioning. Therefore the presence of the steady state conditions had to be
visually assessed for each experiment, through investigations of the logged temperatures.
When the steady state conditions were achieved the given parameters were measured.
The measuring phase for each experiment lasted for approximately 5 hours. Therefore the
calculated energy balance of the room was based on the calculated mean values instead of
113
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 5.
DISCUSSION
an instantaneously measured value. The procedure was chosen because it was important to
eliminate the eﬀect of ﬂuctuations. Afterwards the heat ﬂux were calculated for 10 surfaces,
being the top, bottom and the external vertical walls above and below the plenum. By
doing so, the energy balance were calculated with an unaccounted heat ﬂux below 97 watt,
for all experiments, except for the ”8 person high load 2 exhaust with bass system”, which
had an unaccounted heat ﬂux of -152 watt. The deviations were thought to be caused by
the simpliﬁcations of e.g. uniform surface temperatures of the chamber, deviation of the
measuring equipment or inaccuracy of the voltage supplying the thermal manikins.
114
==PAGE:125==
6.
Conclusion
The application of the diﬀuse ventilation principle showed a potential of obtaining a suf-
ﬁcient IEQ for the diﬀerent scenarios. It is known that the diﬀuse ventilation principle is
strongly dependent on having a buoyancy forced ﬂow[14]. By assessing the Archimedes
number, it can be concluded that the experiments were performed in accordance to these
conditions.
The variation of the load showed a diﬀerence in the ﬂow patterns in the room. When
having a low load layout, the plumes generated by the occupants where shown to be the
dominating factor, by creating upwards velocity vectors adjacent to the thermal manikins.
As the load increased, the tendency was still present, but the stream lines became gradually
more unexpected. Especially the ”8 person high load with 2 exhaust” showed a stream
line pattern, where the expected upward vectors adjacent to the occupants were no longer
present.
Generally, it can be concluded that the models were able to comply with a
complete category A in the occupied zone. The category A were according to; air velocity,
vertical temperature diﬀerence and draught limited to a ”4 person low load” scenario, with
a load of 15W/m2. Compliance with a category B was shown to be limited to an ”8 person
low load” scenario with a load of 30W/m2. However, by applying the extra bass system,
the ”4 person high load w/bass”, with a load of 40W/m2, also managed to comply with a
category B, for the stated parameters. Lastly the limitation for a category C classiﬁcation
was determined by the ”4 person high load” scenarios, with a load of 40W/m2.
Based on the experiments, it can be concluded that the exceedance of categorisation of the
parameters; air velocity, vertical temperature diﬀerence and draught was investigated to
be initiated somewhere within the interval of 40-72W/m2 for the tested scenarios, which
thereby deﬁnes the limitations.
The most aﬀected parameter across the experiments was the air velocity. For some of the
experiments the air velocities were investigated to exceed a category A and B. Generally
this exceedance were only occurring for the high load scenarios. Unfortunately some of the
experiments also showed an air velocity exceeding the categorisation[20], but the majority
of this tendency occurred outside the occupied zone. The most stable parameter was the
air temperature, where the investigated vertical temperature diﬀerence complied with a
category A, in almost all of the scenarios. The only positions where the category A were
exceeded, were just below the ceiling.
The investigation of showing if one or two exhaust diﬀusers would have an impact on
the ﬂow distribution, showed a minimal and not signiﬁcant eﬀect, even for the high load
115
==PAGE:126==
CHAPTER 6.
CONCLUSION
experiments. Therefore the possibility of avoiding draught by having either one or two
exhaust diﬀusers is rejected.
The installation of the sound absorbing plates were measured to have a minimal eﬀect on
the IEQ. It should be notiﬁed that this conclusion is based on two experimental investiga-
tions, whereas the measurement points were strongly reduced in the middle of the room,
for the ”8 person high load experiment”, because of the given arrangement. However, one
experiment showed an ability of obtaining a better categorisation by application of the
extra bass system.
Initially the diﬀuse ventilation principle was said to be a suﬃcient match for high load
environments like oﬃces and schools. It can be concluded that environments like these
would have a tendency of creating conditions for a highly buoyancy forced ﬂow regime,
which is the optimal conditions for the diﬀuse ventilation principle. However, our results
showed that the load pr. square meter, has to be considered in order not to exceed the
categorisation. Because of the consequences by having a unsatisfying indoor environmental
climate[26], it is advised to design buildings according to a category A. Based on our results
the load pr. m2 should not increase more than 15W/m2. This might not seams as much,
so therefore the appliance of a fan coil above the ceiling might not be the best combination
with diﬀuse ceiling, because of the increased air change rate of the room.
116
==PAGE:127==
7.
Further investigation
• Investigation of the exact load where the diﬀuse ventilation principle reaches its
limitation.
• Investigate the eﬀect of supplying air with a low temperature e.g. 5◦C to the plenum,
in order to assess the potential for hybrid ventilation.
• Investigate the heating potential by the diﬀuse ventilation principle.
117
==PAGE:128==
8.
Learning Objectives
By having accomplished this project, the students have learned the following objectives:
• Perform laboratory investigations and understanding how to plan and perform ex-
periments.
• Development of simpliﬁed computational ﬂuid dynamic (CFD) models and the fol-
lowing validation process through comparison with full scale experiments.
• Use of instruments to measure typical indoor environmental parameters like temper-
ature, air velocity and CO2.
• Evaluation of the indoor climate by the collected data.
• Behaviour in a workshop.
• Applying tools in the workshop too produce necessary components.
• Coordination of tasks.
• Presentation of results.
• Understand how the energy is transferred from e.g. heat sources to the environment.
• Handling and adjustment of a test facility through practical understanding and the-
ory.

9.1.3
Calculation of the overall heat transfer rate
The documentation of the calculated overall heat transfer rates were taken from the re-
port Investigations and Development of Capillary Thermal Ceiling Systems with Integrated
Ventilation[16]. Afterwards the heat transfer coeﬃcients were weighted according to the
area of the given heat transfer coeﬃcient.
